Part II · Chapter 8

STORIES & PARABLES

Teaching Through the Heart's Ear

Stories and parables shared by Urs — from the Jataka Tales to the murid who stopped drinking — teach through the heart's ear with warmth and humor.

13 Entries · Urs Qutbuddin Schellenberg

✿

Story of Ayaz - Teaching on Gratitude

C11 · November 21, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

As told by Urs, attributed to Pir Zia's telling of a Murshid story, to illuminate the first aspect of prayer — gratitude:

The Story

"Ayaz was a young man who worked in the palace of the king Mahmud, who saw so much integrity in his eyes that he kept promoting him. Ayaz had come from humble origins, but eventually he became the prime minister."

"Some of the other workers in the palace became envious, and they looked for the chance to bring him down. They saw that every day he would quietly go into the treasury, and they thought: 'That's it. Now we've got him.'"

"So they went to Mahmud and said, 'We're so sorry to tell you this, but you can't trust Ayaz. He's stealing from you. He keeps going into the vault of the treasury.' So Mahmud said, 'I can't believe that of him. The only way I would ever believe such a thing would be if I saw it with my own eyes.'"

"They made a hole in the wall through which Mahmud could look into the treasury, and there he waited and watched as Ayaz went inside. He saw Ayaz opening a chest. And out of this chest, he took a darp — an old frock, a rag cloth. He took off his jeweled crown, his turban, he took off his brocade robe, and he put that old frock on."

"And he stood in front of a mirror and said: 'Oh Ayaz, this is where you come from. This is who you've been. And everything else — the jewels, the silk, the power, the fame — all of that, it isn't yours. That is all the gift of Sultan Mahmud. So remember this, Ayaz. Remember the kindness of the one who has shown you so much generosity.'"

"One can't imagine what went through Mahmud's mind. He was ashamed of what he did. 'How could I have doubted him?' Feeling a bit ashamed to be there peering through the hole, having succumbed to skepticism, but at the same time now being so deeply moved at this amazing soul who so deeply honored the gifts he had bestowed."

"And he felt such a profound love for Ayaz that he rushed into the treasury and embraced him and said: 'They told me that you were stealing from me, but the truth is that you've stolen my heart.'"

Teaching

The story illuminates the heart of gratitude: not sentiment, but a living practice of remembering origin, honoring gift, and staying humble before the source of all one has been given.

• • •

Story of Two Buddhist Monks - Carrying Burdens

C12 · November 21, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

A teaching story on concentration as the threshold between carrying and releasing, as told by Urs:

The Story

"It's the abbot and a young apprentice. They have both vowed that they would uphold the rules of the monastery. And one rule was not to touch a woman."

"They were on their way to another monastery and were walking for days, and they came to a river. And at this river stood a woman asking for help to cross the river. The abbot immediately offered his help, asked the woman to get up on his shoulders, and he carried her over the river so she didn't get wet. He let her down at the other side and gave his thanks, and they parted their ways."

"The two monks walked on, and the young monk was first slightly irritated. Why did he so willfully break the rule of the monastery not to touch a woman? The longer they went in silence, the more he got angry. He's the abbot. He should know better. Why did he do that? He can't be doing that. What an example is he? And so it went on and on in his head."

"After about an hour of silence, it burst out of him and he said to the abbot: 'Why did you do that? Why did you take this woman and help her over the river?' And he let go all his steam. And the abbot just said: 'How interesting. I put the woman down the other side of the river, but you have been carrying her all this way.'"

The Teaching

This story illustrates concentration as the threshold between carrying and releasing - showing the difference between engaging with a situation in the moment versus carrying it mentally long after it has passed.

• • •

Story of the Pious Man Who Tried to Be Moses

C13 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

The Story

"There is a wonderful story of a very pious man whose ideal was Moses. And he knew everything about Prophet Moses, peace be upon him. He tried to be exactly how Moses was — to clothe himself like him, to speak like him, to act like him, to follow the rules as he did. He did this for his whole life."

"And when he finally passed away and came to the doors of heaven, he was sure that he would be greeted very delightfully. And the angel at the door looked at him and said: 'Oh, it seems you are Moses — but Moses is already here. We're actually expecting you.'"

Teaching Point

The story is the answer to the question: what is the work only you can do? The angel was not waiting for another Moses. The angel was waiting for that specific, unrepeatable soul — in its own form, its own note, its own expression.

Context

This story was told in connection with the rule "Do not spare yourself in the work you must accomplish," illustrating that the work we must accomplish is the unique expression of our own divine nature, not an imitation of someone else's path.

• • •

Iblis Wakes the Pious Man

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

"There was a pious person who followed the daily prayers meticulously — he never missed a single prayer. And one day, being more tired than usual, he seemed to oversleep the early morning prayer."

"But there was a mystical being by his bedside, waking him up so he didn't miss the prayer time. And he woke up and looked at this being and was astonished. He was expecting an angel. But there in front of him was actually a fallen jinn — Iblis, Satan, who had woken him up."

"And he asked: 'Why did you wake me up so I don't miss my daily prayer?' And Satan said: 'I knew if you had missed this prayer and woken up, you would have shed a tear that would have been so pure that it would have washed the sins of the earth.'"

Teaching Context:

The story illuminates the teaching on repentance and the convergence of tears — joy, sorrow, and regret meeting in a single moment of the soul. That tear, had it fallen, would have been of a purity so absolute that even the adversary feared its power.

• • •

Murshid's Murid the Alcoholic

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

"It is said that when Murshid asked a murid if he would want to take initiation — because he had attended many teaching sessions and many meetings — the murid said: 'Oh no, I could never.'"

"'Why?' 'Well, you know, I'm a really bad man and after all, I'm an alcoholic. I'm really bound to the drinks, so I could never take initiation.' Murshid asked him: 'And if you would just let go of the drinking when you are in my presence — would that be a possibility?' And he said: 'Yes, of course. I would never drink in your presence.'"

"So he went, and as the story goes, he never drank another day — because he realized that he was always in Murshid's presence."

Urs's Commentary:

"Sometimes these stories might be uplifting and sometimes they might put pressure on us — because this is not how we experience our life. I think, as with many of these teachings, they are a very true and sincere invitation to stay in conversation with our soul. Be as much in conversation with this hidden mystery, with your life's purpose — in a gentle and loving way. Not with the pressure of 'I should be like these beings in these stories,' but with a loving and curious conversation with your soul."

• • •

The Distracted Lady

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

"There was a lady who had no concentration in her life, no focus. She was always prone to distraction, never paying attention. And she went on a journey, and at a certain point she wasn't sure which direction she should follow. So she asked someone who happened to be standing beside the path."

"That person said: 'Once you climb up this hill, there's a cliff, and right beside that cliff, there's a tree. And you can either go right or left, but you should go right.' Now, this lady was prone to distractions. So she asked for directions, but while the person was giving directions, she just stared looking around — half listening to what she had been given."

"She did remember there was something about getting to a tree. So she climbed up the hill, got to a tree, and there were two paths — one to the right, one to the left. And she thought: 'Now what did that person say? Was it the left or the right? It was definitely one of the two. I can't remember which one. I don't want to go the wrong direction. So I think I'll just go straight.'"

"To go straight in that case meant that she actually had to climb up the tree. So she started climbing — went higher and higher into a very high branch. That branch bent down, and then she was hanging by that branch over the cliff. And she was stuck there for a while."

"Then someone came along — a lady riding on an elephant — and she passed right under the branch. Here finally was the chance for the distracted lady to get some help. The lady on the elephant reached up to help her down. She took hold of her hand — but was so distracted that she forgot to let go of the branch. She was just holding on to the lady on the elephant and holding on to the branch."

"So the lady on the elephant got pulled off the elephant, and then just the two of them were hanging there over the cliff on the branch. They were really stuck. Hours passed. There was no help. And finally, to while away the time, the lady who had been riding on the elephant decided to sing a song. And the distracted lady was so delighted by the song that she clapped her hands in applause."

"And of course, that meant letting go of the branch — and so they both tumbled down into the river. And that's the end of the story."

Urs noted: "There's no happy ending, I'm afraid." This is intentional. The story's humor contains a real warning.

"It's a reminder that distraction can get us in a lot of trouble. It's a story that encourages attention. Whatever we're doing, whatever's going on — be present, be aware, listen."

• • •

The Master's Test - Myself is Watching

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

From the Jataka Tales (Buddhist teaching stories)

"'I am poor and weak,' said the master one day to his pupils. 'But you are young and I teach you. It is therefore your duty to find the money which your old teacher needs to live.' 'How can we do so?' asked the pupils. 'The people of this town are so little generous that it would be vain to ask them for help.'"

"'My children,' replied the teacher, 'there is a way to gain money — not by asking, but by taking. It would be no sin for us to steal, for we deserve money more than others. But alas, I am too old and too weak to do it.' 'We are young,' replied the pupils. 'We can do it. There is nothing we would not do for you, dear master. Tell us only how to act and we will obey.'"

"'Young people you are,' said the master. 'It would be nothing to you to seize a rich man's purse. This is the way to do it. Choose a quiet spot where no one watches. Then catch hold of that passerby and take his money. But do not harm him.' 'Straight away we go,' said all the pupils — except one who had been silent, his eyes cast downward."

"The teacher looked at this youth and said: 'My other pupils are courageous and eager to help. But little do you mind your teacher's suffering?' 'Forgive me, master,' he replied. 'The plan you have explained seems to me impossible. That is the reason for my silence.' 'Why is it impossible?' asked the master."

"'Because there is no place where no one is watching,' replied the pupil. 'Even when I am quite alone — by myself — myself is watching. I would rather take a bowl and beg than allow myself to see me stealing.'"

"At these words, the master's face lighted up with joy. And he took the young pupil in his arms and embraced him. 'Happy am I,' he said, 'if among my pupils, one has understood my words.' His other pupils, seeing that their master had meant to test them, bent their heads in shame. And after that day, whenever an unworthy thought came to their minds, they remembered their companion's words: 'Myself is watching.' Thus became great human beings, and they all lived happily ever after."

Urs's Commentary:

"Myself is watching."

"This story also reminds us of the distinctions between our outer role we play and who we are in private. The stories we heard are not so much about who we sometimes need to appear as in the work we do or what people expect us to be. It is much more linked to our soul and the being we're ever becoming — we've always been, we always will be, but which is on a path."

• • •

Baba Farid Shankar and the Cow

C16 · November 23, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

A teaching story about Baba Farid Ganj-e-Shakar (c. 1173–1266), the great Chishti saint, here as a young murid of Khwaja Qutbuddin Bakhtiyar Kaki. This story demonstrates the power — and comedy — of complete concentration and sympathy.

The Story

"The murshid had many murids, and they gathered with Khwaja Qutbuddin to receive instructions. He said: 'I would like each of you to think of something that you find lovely, something that you find beautiful. Think of it. Go off into a little cell, a little room, and think of something you really love. Take a while — then I'll call you back.'"

"So they each went their way into their little rooms. Baba Farid went into his cell, and he thought for a moment: 'What do I really like?' And then he thought of his cow. His cow back home — a very sweet animal he had always had a special affection for."

"'That cow is really such a splendid creature. I think I will think about that cow.' So he went into concentration."

"Then the time came when all the murids had to be called back, and they all came back pretty quickly — except Baba Farid. He just stayed in that room. So the murshid went to the door and said: 'All right, it's time to come back now. Baba Farid, come on.' And Baba Farid answered: 'Moo... Moo...'"

"So the murshid said: 'No, no, no — that's over now. You can open your eyes. We've finished. Come on.' Then Baba Farid got up and came to the door — but each time he was about to pass through, he stopped."

"The murshid said: 'Come on now, we've got to come back.' And Baba Farid said: 'Horns too big. The horns too big.' He couldn't get through the door because of his big horns."

"So he had really given himself to the practice. And not only did he think about the cow — he became the cow."

• • •

The Patient Buffalo - Jataka Tales

C16 · November 23, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

A teaching story from the Jataka Tales, attributed to Pir Sad Nur. A teaching on endurance, the thinking mind, and the patience that calls forth grace.

The Story

"A giant-like buffalo with mighty horns lay under a tree asleep. Two mischievous eyes peeped through the branches. And a little monkey said: 'I know a good old buffalo who's sleeping 'neath the tree, but I am not afraid of him, nor he's afraid of me.' And he leaped from the branch on the buffalo's neck."

"The buffalo opened his eyes, and seeing the monkey dancing on his hip, he closed them again as if only a butterfly were on his back."

"Then the rascal monkey tried another trick — jumping on the buffalo's head between his two large horns. He held the ends and swung as on a tree. The buffalo did not even wink. 'What can I do to make this good friend angry?' he thought."

"While the buffalo was eating in the fields, the monkey trampled on the grass wherever he wished to graze. And the buffalo merely walked away. Another day, the mischievous monkey took a stick and knocked the buffalo's ears with it. Then, while he was taking a walk, sat on his back like a hero holding the stick in his hand. To all of this, the buffalo never made a murmur — though his horns were strong and mighty."

"But one day, while the monkey sat on his back, a fairy appeared — a great being. 'You old buffalo,' she said, 'but little do you know your strength. Your horns can break down trees, and your feet could crush rocks. Lions and tigers fear your approach. Your strength and beauty are known to the whole world. And yet you walk about with a foolish monkey on your back. One blow of your horn would pierce him and a stroke of your foot would crush him. Why do you not throw him on the ground? Finish with this play.'"

"'The monkey is small,' replied the buffalo, 'and nature has not given him much brain. Why then should I punish him? Moreover, why should I make him suffer in order that I may be happy?' At this the fairy smiled, and with her magic wand she drove the monkey away, and she gave the great buffalo a charm by which no one could ever cause him to suffer again, and they lived happily ever after."

Commentary - The Monkey as the Thinking Mind

"In Buddhist tales, the monkey often represents the thinking mind. And it sometimes feels like this when we're sitting and doing our concentration practices — this mind is like the monkey who's trying new and new tricks to distract us."

"But in the story, it is said that the monkey refers to the buffalo as his friend. He's not treating him like this because he doesn't like him. There is love for the buffalo, there is a dependence on it."

"And on the other hand, the buffalo says: 'I do not need my mighty strength to crush the monkey. What would it give me? What joy would it give me?'"

"By this endurance, by this knowing his true strength and letting the monkey be the monkey without giving him more attention than necessary — by not taking the bait, not putting his concentration and awareness to the monkey — by that, the fairy appeared and freed him."

"So don't take the bait and try to stay focused. It can also be sometimes even entertaining to see how clever the mind is to get your attention — and there is something lovable about it. But we don't stay there with our attention."

Teaching Point:

The buffalo's approach to the monkey — patient, un-reactive, rooted in its own nature — is the practice of concentration itself. Not suppression, not combat, not feeding. Just the return, again and again, to the point of concentration. The fairy who appears is grace: it comes not from fighting the monkey but from the quality of patient, dignified presence.

• • •

The Story of Moses and the Shepherd (Rumi)

C17 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

From Rumi's Masnavi, taught in the context of invocation and the many valid forms of prayer:

The Story

"Moses, the Prophet Moses, peace be upon him, was walking in the desert and there he saw a shepherd who was calling on God. And the shepherd was saying: 'Oh God, if you would only come to me, I would darn your socks and wash your cloth, and comb your hair, and kill your lice, and give you milk to drink, and massage your little hands and feet.'"

"Now, hearing this, Moses was greatly taken aback. He said to the shepherd: 'Who do you think you're talking to? Your uncle? You're speaking to the creator of the whole universe, and the words you are using are poorly chosen.' Now the shepherd was struck by these words of Moses and quietly went off to the distance."

"But then a revelation came to Moses and the divine voice spoke and said: 'Moses, you have come between a worshipper and me. Your mission is to bring union, not separation — but you've caused a separation here. Every person, every worshipper has their voice, has their way of expression, and one cannot be for another. Each uses the words that come naturally to themselves. And this was his way of speaking.'"

"'But I don't look at the words. I look at what is in the heart. I want to see burning. Burning is what I hear.'"

"So Moses realized that he had made a mistake. And he chased after the shepherd and said: 'News has come. Wait, wait, just listen. Give your heart's prayer to God.'"

The Shepherd's Response

"And the shepherd looked at him and said: 'I've gone beyond that now.'"

The disturbance itself became the catalyst for a further ascent. Moses's rebuke — though mistaken in its form — became the force that pushed the shepherd beyond the rung he had been on. What looked like harm was in fact, within the divine economy, a grace.

Teaching Points

  • Every worshipper has their own authentic voice and way of expression
  • God looks not at the words but at the burning in the heart
  • Sometimes apparent obstacles become catalysts for spiritual advancement
  • The mission of prophets is to bring union, not separation
  • Even mistakes in guidance can serve the divine purpose
• • •

Story of the Door Knock - "It's Me" → "It's You"

C19 · December 14, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Classic Sufi teaching story on union, often attributed to Rumi:

"There is a wonderful story that is often told to describe this union. It tells of a person looking for her friend, going to a door, knocking at the door of her friend. And the voice says: 'Who is there?' And she says: 'It's me, your friend.' The voice says: 'There is only room for one in here, so go away.'"

"And after a long time and many years, she returns, knocks again at the door. And the voice says: 'Who is there?' And she says: 'It's you, beloved.' And the door opens. There is only room for one."

"There is only room for one."

This story is the fifth aspect of prayer crystallized in a single image: the journey from 'It's me' — duality, the self presenting itself — to 'It's you' — the dissolution of the self into the presence of the beloved. The door that could not open for the separated self opens immediately for the one who has become the beloved.

• • •

The Dervish Who Called Everyone 'Ya Murshid'

C20 · December 14, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

The Story

This story comes from Murshid's own life when he was with his own murshid:

Hazrat Inayat Khan sees a dervish approaching him. The dervish says: "Ya Murshid! Oh Murshid! Oh Murshid! My Murshid!"

Hazrat Inayat Khan is uplifted, sure that this dervish sees the resonance of his own murshid in him. That's why he's calling him Murshid.

But then he sees the dervish wander off and go to the next person — a policeman — saying: "Ya Murshid! Oh Murshid! Oh my Murshid!"

Hazrat Inayat Khan realizes, as he describes, that it was not so much the resonance of his own murshid that the dervish saw — but the dervish was so immersed in the image of his own murshid that all he could see, in everybody and everything he saw, he saw his murshid.

The Teaching

This story illustrates the third stage of fana-fi-sheikh — where the murshid is no longer an image held separately, but becomes the lens through which all reality is perceived.

The dervish had reached a state where the murshid was no longer "other" but had become his very way of seeing. The policeman becomes the murshid because the dervish sees nothing but the murshid.

This is the living reality of complete absorption in the ideal — where the boundary between self and teacher dissolves so completely that the entire world becomes a theophany of the beloved guide.

• • •

The Little Hare and the End of the World

C20 · December 14, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

From the Jataka Tales

The Story

One day, a little hare sat under a fruit tree and thought, and thought, and thought: "What will happen to me when the earth comes to an end?" And at that very moment, a fruit fell from the tree. Off ran little hare as fast as his legs could carry him, so sure he was that the noise of the fruit falling was that of the earth breaking to pieces.

"Brother, brother!" called another little hare who saw him running. "Pray, tell me what has happened?" But little hare ran on and did not even turn to answer. The other hare ran after him. At last, little hare stopped a moment and said: "The earth is breaking to pieces." At this, the other hare started running still faster. A third hare joined, and a fourth, and a fifth — till a hundred thousand hares were racing through the fields.

As they raced through the forest and the deep jungles, the deer, the boars, the elks, the buffalos, the oxen, the rhinoceros, the tigers, the lions, and the elephants — hearing that the earth was coming to an end — all ran wildly with them. But among those living in the jungle was one lion, a wise lion, who knew everything that took place in the world.

When it became known to him that so many hundreds and thousands of animals were running away because they believed the earth was breaking to pieces, he thought: "This earth of ours is far from coming to an end — but my poor creatures will die if I do not save them, for in their fright they will run into the sea."

And he ran at such a pace that he reached a certain mountain in their path before they came to it. As they passed by the mountain, he roared three times with such a mighty roar that they stopped in their mad flight and stood still, trembling. The great lion descended and approached them. "Why are you running at such a pace?" he asked. "The earth is breaking to pieces," they replied.

"Who saw it breaking to pieces?" he asked. "The elephants." "Did you see it breaking?" he asked the elephants. "No. The lions saw it." He asked each in turn — the lions, the tigers, the rhinoceros, the oxen, the buffalo, the elk, the boar, the deer, the hares — until he came to the little hare.

"Did you see the earth breaking?" he asked the little hare. "Yes, Lord," replied the hare. "I was sitting beneath a fruit tree and I thought: what will happen to me when the earth comes to an end? And at that very moment, I heard the noise of the earth breaking and I ran."

The great lion thought: "He was sitting beneath the fruit tree — so certainly the noise he heard was that of a fruit falling." "Ride on my back, little one," he said, "and show me where you saw the earth break." Little hare jumped on his back, and they flew to the place.

But as they approached the fruit tree, little hare jumped off, so frightened he was to return to the spot. He pointed out the tree. The great one went to the tree and saw the spot where little hare had been sitting, and the fruit which had fallen. "Come here, little one — now, where do you see the earth broken?" Little hare, after looking around and seeing the fruit on the ground, knew that there had been no occasion for his fright.

He jumped once again on the lion's back, and away they went to the hundreds and thousands of creatures who were awaiting their return. The lion then told the great multitude that the noise little hare had heard was of a fruit falling to the ground. And so all turned back — the elephants to the jungle, the lions to the caves, the deer to the river banks, and little hare to the fruit tree. And they all lived happy ever after.

On Gentleness with Practice

Amir Minai on Sin and Divine Mercy

C13 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

"As my sins arouse the mercy of my Lord, not to sin is a sin of a sort. As my sins arouse the mercy of my Lord, not to sin is a sin of a sort."

— Amir Minai

Commentary

This paradoxical verse points toward the completeness of divine mercy: even the misstep calls forth something healing from the divine. The universe is not lessened by our falling — it is, in some mysterious way, made more complete.

Context

This verse was shared in connection with the teaching on repentance and the quality of Rahman (the All-Compassionate), illustrating the mysterious workings of divine mercy.

Urs Personal Moment - Fire of Anger as Teaching

C15 · November 23, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

★ Urs shared a real-time personal experience of fire — rare transparency, deliberately offered as teaching:

"I had the good fortune today to be visited by this fire. I, by accident, opened a message from my work, and immediately I could feel the fire rising in me. Fire that makes you alert, makes your mind very present — but also sets the stage to be destructive, to be in this fire as a form of anger."

"And when it happened, I wasn't so thankful yet. But I could really feel how my body was heated up. And that was so amazing — that a simple word can spark the fire in you."

"Maybe you have experienced similar things during these days — that when we immerse in one of these elements, they come to visit. And when they visit, I hope we can acknowledge it and be with it, and when we've settled again, be with this atmosphere of this element."

Teaching Point

This transparency models precisely what is being taught: the elements are not abstract or distant. When we work with them, they arrive. Acknowledging the fire rather than suppressing it — and then settling into the element's full quality — is the practice itself.

From Urs Qutbuddin's valediction at the end of the November weekend:

"I wish you a wonderful time with your practice. Be gentle, but focused, and explore with joy. Take everything like nectar that's given to you, everything you find."

Context: This instruction was given as students prepared to return to daily life after the intensive weekend of concentration practices. It suggests an attitude of receptivity and gratitude toward all experiences encountered in spiritual practice and ordinary life.

Five Poems from the Gayan/Vadan - Munajat

C17 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Five complete poems read by Urs from Murshid's Gayan/Vadan as munajat — the lover's address to the beloved — in the register of invocation, the air aspect of prayer:

Poem I — Thou Pourest Wine

Thou pourest wine into my empty cup.

Wherever we meet — on hills and dales,

on the tops of the high mountains,

in the thick forests, and in the barren deserts,

on the shores of the roaring sea,

and on the banks of the gentle river —

there arises in my heart the unearthly passion

and the heavenly joy.

Poem II — In the Swinging of the Branches

In the swinging of the branches,

in the flying of the birds,

and in the running of the water,

beloved, I see thy waving hand bidding me goodbye.

Poem III — In the Cooling of the Wind

In the cooling of the wind,

in the roaring of the sea,

and in the crashing of the thunder,

beloved, I see thee weep and I hear thy cry.

Poem IV — In the Promise of the Dawn

In the promise of the dawn,

in the breaking of the morn,

in the smiles of the rose,

beloved, I see thy joy at my homecoming.

Every step in thy path draws me nearer to thee.

Every breath in thy thought exhilarates my spirit.

Every glimpse of thy smile is inspiring to my soul.

Every tear in thy love, beloved, exalts my being.

Poem V — Every Form I See · Wherever I Roam

Every form I see is thine own form, my Lord.

And every sound I hear is thine own voice.

In the perfume of flowers, I perceive the fragrance of thy spirit.

In every word spoken to me, I hear thy voice, my Lord.

All that touches me is thine own touch.

In everything I taste, I enjoy the savour of thy delicious spirit.

In every place, I feel thy presence, beloved.

In every word that falls on my ears, I hear thy message.

Everything that touches me thrills me with the joy of thy kiss.

Wherever I roam, I meet thee.

Wherever I reach, I find thee, my Lord.

Wherever I look, I see thy glorious vision.

Whatever I touch, I touch thy beloved hand.

Whosoever I see, I see thee in his soul.

Whoever ought gives to me, I take it from thee.

To whomsoever I give, I humbly offer it to thee, Lord.

Whoever comes to me, it is thou who comest.

On whomsoever I call, I call on thee.

• • •

Key Quotes from Urs - Session C17

C17 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

On Suluk Community Support

"There is a reason why we don't travel alone."

"Let us support each other in our prayers. When we are in our prayers, let's think of each other. And even though we might not know each and every situation our fellow travelers are in — just be in the presence of this group and wish everyone well."

On the Living World

"We are not surrounded by dead matter, but we are within an immense living body. And the elements on the outside are like our siblings, our brothers and sisters."

On Crystalline Spirit

"You could say the elements, our bodies, and our surrounding are crystalline spirit — spirit that became form. And spirit being the abstraction of matter."

On the God-Ideal

"It's like the north star. It's our picture of perfection — one that we hold now, and that we let go when this rung of the ladder has helped us to rise further."

On Music as Teaching

"I just would like to point out that the music which might seem like an introduction to the teaching is actually very much at the core of the teaching itself."

On Individual Prayer Voices

"Every person, every worshipper has their voice, has their way of expression, and one cannot be for another. Each uses the words that come naturally to themselves."

Divine Voice on Burning (from the Moses story)

"I don't look at the words. I look at what is in the heart. I want to see burning. Burning is what I hear."

• • •

Four Murshid Passages - The Soul's Journey to Union

C19 · December 14, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Four complete passages read by Urs from Murshid — the soul's journey from prostration before God to calling God 'myself.'

Passage I — From Lord to Myself

When thou didst sit upon thy throne with a crown upon thy head,

I did prostrate myself upon the ground and call thee my Lord.

When thou didst stretch out thy hands in blessing over me,

I knelt and called thee my master.

When thou didst raise me from the ground, holding me with thine arms,

I drew closer to thee and called thee my beloved.

But when thy caressing hands held my head next to thy glowing heart

and thou didst kiss me, I smiled and called thee myself.

Passage II — Why Did I Not Recognize Thee?

Why did I not recognize thee when first I opened my eyes on the earth?

Why did I not respond to thee when I heard thy enchanting voice?

Why did I not feel thy gentle hand when thou didst caress my face?

Why did I not cling to thee, beloved, when thou lovingly didst kiss my lips?

When I began to look for thee, in the twinkling of an eye, thou didst disappear.

When I started in thy pursuit, thou didst move away from me still farther.

When I called thee aloud in my distress, thou didst not hear my soul's bitter cry.

Cross-legged I sat in silence, then alone I heard thy call.

Passage III — The Search (Minaret, Ganges, Ka'aba)

I searched but I could not find thee.

I called thee aloud standing on the minaret.

I rang the temple bell with the rising and setting of the sun.

I bathed in the Ganges in vain. I came back from the Ka'aba disappointed.

I looked for thee on the earth. I searched for thee in the heaven, my beloved,

but at last have found thee hidden as a pearl in the shell of my heart.

Passage IV — I Must Call Thee Myself

Do I call thee my soul, but thou art my spirit.

Can I call thee my life, but thou livest forever.

May I call thee my beloved, but thou art love itself.

Then what must I call thee? I must call thee myself.

✿ ✿ ✿
Part II · Chapter 10

DEEP STUDY & ARABIC TERMS

The Language of the Path

Arabic terms, elemental vocabulary, and deeper learnings from Urs's sessions. Includes Sufi terminology related to the elements, the body, and the murshid-murid relationship.

14 Entries · Urs Qutbuddin Schellenberg

✿

Eshk - Love and Longing with Earth

C11 · November 21, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Term: Eshk (عشق)

Context: Earth communion practice

Definition: In Sufi usage, a consuming, longing love. Here Urs applies it not to divine-human love but to the primal bond between embodied being and the Earth that holds it.

Usage in Teaching

"This communion of the Earth and our bodies is also described as eshk — a form of love and longing."

"Be aware of this eshk when you take your next step. Your next step might be like a kiss — your body kissing the Earth."

Related Practice

The term was introduced in connection with the Earth element practice: developing conscious awareness and communion with Earth both as outer support and as the very substance of our embodied being.

• • •

Shukr - Gratitude in Prayer

C11 · November 21, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Term: Shukr

Context: Five Aspects of Prayer - First Aspect

Definition: Gratitude, thankfulness - the first aspect of prayer as described by Murshid in his work on the universality of religious ideals.

Teaching Context

"The first aspect is very close to Earth and what we've been experiencing — and that is gratitude. The aspect of gratitude."

Two Modes Distinguished

Social gratitude — saying thank you, seeing the other, acknowledging what they've given

Cosmic gratitude — thankfulness for everything dispersed into existence: the air, the Earth, the warmth, the fruit

Practice Application

"So this aspect of prayer is an invitation to turn our attention, our focus, our concentration on the things that deserve our gratitude."

Related Zikr: Ashukrillah - "Thanks be to the One"

• • •

Uruj and Nuzul - Rising and Descending

C12 · November 21, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Key Arabic terms from Murshid's teaching on breath:

Uruj (عروج) — rising; the in-breath and its inward, ascending movement

Nuzul (نزول) — descending; the out-breath and its outward, expressing movement

Eshk (عشق) — love and longing; used in C11 for Earth communion; echoed here

Context

"Inhaling is called 'uruj,' which means rising. Exhaling is called 'nuzul,' which means descending. And each has its particular part in the life of an individual." — Murshid

• • •

Lataif and the Breath of Life - Reviving Subtle Centers

C13 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Water Quality and the Breath of Life

"This water quality is compared to the breath of life — which has, like the water, the ability through its flow to wash through blockades in our subtle centers, in the Lataif, cleaning them, freeing them from residues."

The Barren Land and the Rain

"When we look out and see dry land that hasn't seen water for a while — maybe for a month — no life seems to be with it. And the day the clouds are slowly shifted over this land and the rain pours down, it doesn't take long until this life is seen. The first offshoots of green are seen, miraculously — held by the earth and revivified by water."

Reviving Dormant Subtle Centers

"And this is the same with our breath. When there are parts in us, our subtle centers, that seem to be barren — with the breath of life, they start greening again."

"And maybe this is something that's happening to us in these days: parts in us that didn't get much attention, that were dormant, held by the earth element — show their shoots and slowly become a plant again."

Connection to Khwaja Khidr

"There are those marvelous beings that can create greenery around them — like Khwaja Khidr, who, it is said, when he stepped the earth, the plant started to grow at that point."

Context

This teaching connects the water elemental breath practice to the activation and cleansing of the Lataif (subtle centers), showing how breath work can revive dormant aspects of our spiritual being.

• • •

Rahman - The All-Compassionate as Healing Quality

C13 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

"The quality of Rahman is always there, unconditionally. The quality of Rahman also is living in the human heart — in our ability to forgive and let go and to love. It's a healing aspect."

Context

This teaching on Rahman was given in connection with the practice of repentance and working with regret, showing how the divine quality of compassion exists both as an attribute of God and as a potential within the human heart.

The practice suggested bringing unresolved regret "before the one, the all-compassionate: 'Let it be held in his loving compassion.'"

• • •

Bodhisattva

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Definition: In Buddhist teaching, a being on the path to enlightenment who delays full liberation to serve the liberation of all beings.

Context from Teaching: Encountered in Stage 3 of the three-stage being visualization in its pre-earthly, non-physical form.

Related Terms: Buddha, enlightenment, non-physical beings

• • •

Iblis (إبليس)

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Definition: Satan; the Adversary in Islamic theology; a jinn who refused to bow to Adam and was cast out.

Context from Teaching: In the story of the pious man, Iblis acts paradoxically to prevent the pure tear — showing that even the adversary recognizes the power of sincere repentance.

Related Terms: Jinn, Satan

• • •

Jataka Tales

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Definition: A collection of Buddhist teaching stories recounting the Buddha's previous lives and moral lessons.

Context from Teaching: The source of 'The Master's Test' story featuring the student who says "Myself is watching" - illustrating the inner witness that observes all actions.

Related Terms: Buddhist teachings, teaching stories

• • •

Jinn (جن)

C14 · November 22, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Definition: Non-human, non-angelic beings in Islamic cosmology; created from smokeless fire; occupy a spectrum from fallen (like Iblis) to illuminated.

Context from Teaching: Encountered in Stage 3 of the three-stage visualization as a non-physical being that has never existed on the physical plane.

Related Terms: Iblis, angel, non-physical beings

• • •

Fana-fi-Sheikh and Related Terms

C16 · November 23, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Fana-fi-Sheikh (فناء في الشيخ) — annihilation in the teacher; the Sufi practice of effacing the personal self in the presence of one's murshid, thereby receiving the qualities and light of the entire lineage (Silsila) through that living embodiment

Silsila (سلسلة) — the chain of transmission; the unbroken lineage of teachers through which the light and baraka (blessing) of the tradition is passed from heart to heart, generation to generation

Fana (فناء) — annihilation; the dissolving of the individual self. In Fana-fi-Sheikh, it is specifically the self dissolved into the murshid's being, as a preparatory stage before Fana-fillah (annihilation in God)

Baba Farid Ganj-e-Shakar (1173–1266) — one of the great saints of the Chishti order; disciple of Khwaja Qutbuddin Bakhtiyar Kaki; known as 'the treasury of sugar'; his shrine in Pakpattan, Pakistan, remains a major site of pilgrimage

Khwaja Qutbuddin Bakhtiyar Kaki — Chishti master, disciple of Moinuddin Chishti (Khwaja Gharib Nawaz); murshid of Baba Farid; one of the principal figures of the Chishti order's establishment in the Indian subcontinent

Khatum — the closing prayer of the Inayatiyya; 'O Thou who art the perfection of love, harmony and beauty'; used to seal gatherings and retreat sessions

Inter esse (Latin) — to be between; to be in the midst of; the etymological root of 'interest'; teaching point: genuine interest is not casual preference but a state of total immersion where self and object merge

• • •

Arabic and Sufi Terms - Session C17

C17 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Ruh al-Azam (روح الأعظم)

The Great Spirit - The first creation; the universal life in which all individual souls exist as expressions. Not the absolute one (Allah), but the primordial divine breath that is the ground of creation.

Munajat (مناجات)

Intimate address to the divine - The lover's cry in the night or wilderness; a form of prayer that is pure longing without petition. Found in classical maqam and raga as a musical register of nearness.

Maqam (مقام)

Station - A spiritual rank or degree of attainment on the path. In the story of the shepherd, he reaches a higher maqam through the very disturbance Moses caused. Also the term for the musical modes of Arab and Persian classical music.

Haiya (حيا)

Supplication - The aspect of prayer as asking for support for one's aims; driven longing for specific fulfillment. Mapped to the Fire element. Distinguished from Invocation (Air) which seeks only nearness.

Shukr (شكر)

Gratitude - The first and most grounded aspect of prayer, mapped to the Earth element.

Istighfar / Tawba (استغفار / توبة)

Forgiveness/Repentance - The cleansing aspect of prayer, mapped to the Water element. Tawba literally means "turning back" to God.

God-ideal

Murshid's term for the personalized conception of God one holds at a given stage of the path. Like the north star: not the destination itself, but the directional image that orients the journey. Meant to be released as one rises.

Gayan Vadan Nirtan

The three principal volumes of Murshid Hazrat Inayat Khan's mystical writings: Gayan (sayings), Vadan (deeper thoughts and meditations), Nirtan (the dance, devotional poetry). The poems in this session are drawn from these.

Salamander

In medieval and alchemical tradition, a creature believed to live within fire unharmed and even able to quench it. A symbol of the purified soul that passes through the fire of transformation without being consumed.

• • •

Elemental Philosophy - Crystalline Spirit and Ruh al-Azam

C17 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Urs offered the most comprehensive philosophical framing of the elemental work, drawing together the full arc of understanding:

The Elements as Living Siblings

"Stepping into the livingness of the world — we are not surrounded by dead matter, but we are within an immense living body. And the elements on the outside are like our siblings, our brothers and sisters."

"We are not even separated from the elements on the outside, as we ourselves are made from these elements. In our flesh and bones, the fluids, blood, the digestive fire, the air filling our lungs with every breath, running through the whole body."

Elements in Character, Spirit, and Soul

In character and temperament: persistence like earth; the moved and open heart like water; inspiration and will like fire

In the spirit: traceable in dreams, as a language of the spirit

In the soul: everything reflected there ultimately

Crystalline Spirit

"You could say the elements, our bodies, and our surrounding are crystalline spirit — spirit that became form. And spirit being the abstraction of matter."

The Pendulum of Awareness

"In our awareness of these dimensions, it's sometimes like a pendulum — sometimes more leaning to the material world, the palpable, the solid, the concrete. And sometimes we're more aware of spirit, the subtle, the very fine. And when you're doing the elemental breath, you might one day be closer to one aspect than to the other. And sometimes it's all there, present at once."

Ruh al-Azam — The Great Spirit

"This brings us closer to the awareness of being a part of the Ruh al-Azam, the great spirit, the great body. The great spirit being the first created thing — not the one beyond everything and all, but this first creation in which we live."

Cleansing and Renewal Through the Elements

"There is this wonderful quality of the elements: of cleansing, of washing us, of taking from us everything that is burdening, that is heavy, that's not used anymore — everything we can let go of. And the elements giving us back new energy, new magnetism, filling us with life again. A renewal of every aspect of us."

• • •

New Terms from Concentration Session

C18 · December 13, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Cupid position — The archer's cross-legged seat used in Zikr; right foot tucked behind the left knee, the tendon behind the kneecap representing the bow's string

Position of Sages (Full Lotus / Padmasana) — Both feet placed on opposite thighs, soles facing upward; powerful but demanding posture for practice

Luminous glance — Murshid's teaching that the eye does not only receive light but sheds it; the glance as a beam of inner light cast outward into the world

Still-point — The moment between the exhale completing and the inhale beginning, where the body's natural magnetism is most palpable

• • •

Glossary - New Terms from Session

C19 · December 14, 2025 · Urs Qutbuddin

Ether / Akash (आकाश) / Asman (آسمان)

The fifth element; the quintessence; sky in Sanskrit and Persian respectively; the primordial space from which all elements emerge and into which all dissolve; no color, no direction, no form — and yet all colors, all directions, all forms in potential

Khamush (خاموش)

Silence; the Persian word Rumi uses to end many poems when language reaches its limit; the fifth aspect of prayer, beyond all words

Ishq (عشق)

Love; the same word as eshk; used here in the fire context as consuming divine love that burns everything except the beloved. The word carries both the gentle longing of earth-eshk (C11) and the consuming fire of divine union.

Minaret

The tower of a mosque from which the call to prayer (adhan) is given; in the third passage, Murshid searched for the beloved by calling from the minaret — the highest external point — and found the beloved only within, in the heart

Khatum — The Closing Prayer

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

"Well Thou who art the perfection of love, harmony and beauty, sovereign of heaven and earth. Open our hearts that we may hear Thy voice, which constantly comes from within. Disclose to us Thy divine light which is hidden in our souls, that we may know and understand life better. Most merciful and compassionate God, give us Thy great goodness. Teach us Thy loving forgiveness, raise us above the distinctions and differences which divide us. Send us the peace of Thy divine spirit, and unite us all in Thy perfect being. Om."

Usage

  • Closing prayer for practice sessions
  • Part of regular daily practice routine
• • •

The Invocation

When reciting the invocation, bring forth the intention and feeling of longing — of union with the One.

It is this inner state that connects the heart to the One. Without it, the invocation becomes only a repetition of words.

The invocation is not the words — it is the state from which the words arise.

BREATH PRACTICES

The Bridge Between Worlds

Breath practices prescribed by Guide Mansur — including Qasab (the reed breath), Hosh dar Dam, and the Five Elemental Purification Breaths in their specific form for Kabir's daily practice.

Elemental Breaths — Observed Benefits and Practice

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

Kabir's Testimony (7 rounds daily)

  • Without practice: brain fog, fatigue, inability to think, wanting to sleep
  • With practice: energy elevated, channels clear, clarity of thought restored

"It's like medicine. I can see the change." — Kabir

Mansur's Framework

  • Elemental Breaths = purification of the body
  • Qasab = purification of the mind
  • Both clear the channels of breath (Nadis — 70,000 in yoga tradition)
  • Can do 1 round even in the car before a difficult meeting

Practice Guidelines

  • 7 rounds each element
  • Maximum 10 rounds (Murshid's guidance)
  • Inhale through mouth, exhale through nose
• • •

Five Elemental Purification Breaths

December 12, 2025 · Mansur

Mansur gave complete guidance on each elemental breath — direction, visualization, and spiritual principle.

Earth Breath

Principle: "Stones are bones and flesh is clay." — Principle of solidity.

Direction: Spreading — toward you from every direction.

Practice:

  • Inhale: Earth spreading toward you; imagine being a mountain buried from waist down
  • Exhale: Allow gravity (the earth's embrace) to purify the earth element

Water Breath

Principle: Pir Vilayat: "This element is very close to the Holy Spirit."

Direction: Down.

Practice:

  • Visualization: Waterfall starting just above the head — high volume, slow-moving water rinsing over and through you

Fire Breath

Principle: Pir Vilayat: "You must burn very hot."

Direction: Upward

Breathing: Inhale through mouth, exhale through nose

Practice:

  • Visualization: Bed of glowing coals in solar plexus/belly
  • Inhale: Like a bellows making coals bright
  • Exhale: As exhaling light. Convection currents rising above the fire

Air Breath

Principle: Atoms are 99.999...% open space — allow the wind to pass through you.

Direction: Every which way / zigzag

Breathing: In mouth, out mouth

Practice:

  • Visualization: Swirling wind washing through you, cleansing like wind through trees

Ether Breath

Principle: The all-pervading life in space — omnidirectional, pervading presence.

Practice:

  • Feel the shimmering energy in the body
  • "Through the rays of the sun, through the waves of the air, through the all-pervading life in space, purify and revivify us... Heal our bodies, hearts and souls." — Prayer of Niyaz

Additional Teachings

Daily practice — ideally outside or before an open window. 5 breaths per element. Full, even, natural, unforced.

ElementPatternDirectionColorQualities
**Earth**In nose · Out noseHorizontal, spreadingGold wheatPatience, quiet strength
**Water**In nose · Out mouthDescendingSea greenGenerosity, creativity
**Fire**In mouth · Out noseRisingRedArdor, passion, truth
**Air**In mouth · Out mouthZigzagSky bluePeace
**Ether**In+out both simultaneouslyWithinUltra-fine grey mistLetting go

Add associated concentrations (direction, color, quality) one at a time — only when they are not a distracting complication.

• • •

Hosh dar Dam — Breath Awareness

Hosh dar dam — "Consciousness on the breath."

Make the breath your home base. Go there whenever your thinking has no meaningful occupation.

This is not a formal sitting practice but a life-practice — the breath as continuous anchor of awareness throughout the day.

Your life becomes your practice.

One of the foundational principles of the Chishti path: the breath as the bridge between the outer and inner life.

• • •

Purification Breath Health Testimony

December 12, 2025 · Mansur

Pir Vilayat's Teaching

Pir Vilayat: "If you do the purification breaths every day, you'll never get seriously ill."

Mansur's Personal Testimony

25 years of daily practice

Sick only twice in 25 years:

  1. Once under extreme life stress (selling home, business, divorce)
  2. Once briefly with COVID in 2022

All other time: completely well.

This stands as powerful testimony to the healing power of consistent daily purification breath practice.

• • •

Qasab — The Reed Breath

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

Hand position: Right middle finger against right nostril, right thumb under chin. Left hand covers right hand, left thumb to close left nostril. Breath is light but deep, without strain.

Sequence (1 round — repeat 1, 3, 5, or 7 times)

  1. Close right nostril → inhale and exhale through left
  2. Close left nostril → inhale and exhale through right
  3. Close right; inhale left → close left; exhale right
  4. Close left; inhale right → close right; exhale left
  5. Close right; inhale left → exhale through mouth
  6. Close left; inhale right → exhale through mouth
  7. Close right; inhale mouth → exhale through left
  8. Close left; inhale mouth → exhale through right
  9. Lower hands

Additional Teachings

Draw as deep a breath as you can without straining.

At the top of inhale:

  • Close nostrils
  • Keep chest expanded (do not close the glottis)
  • Curl tongue along roof of mouth
  • Turn eyeballs upward
  • Hold for 2× the count of the inhale
  • Think HU — or Allah on inhale, Hu on exhale

Part III · Chapter 3

CONCENTRATION, VISUALIZATION & MEDITATION

Training the Sovereign Attention

Concentration and meditation practices from Guide Mansur — including the Sky of Mind practice, Muraqabah (watchfulness of thought), and the Ideal Guru Contemplation.

3 Entries · Guide Mansur

✿

Ideal Guru Contemplation

Hold the archetype of the Ideal Guru using the Sufi ideal that emerged from your own experience.

Remember the being of Pir Zia, as well as the yogi who blessed you long ago.

Such beings manifest and radiate the One according to their revelation.

"It can't be taught, but it can be caught." — Mansur

This beingness is contagious. The contemplation is not intellectual — it is an absorption into the quality of presence that such a being radiates.

• • •

Muraqabah — Watchfulness of Thought

Be watchful of your thoughts.

  • When a negative thought arises, pause. Ask: where is this coming from?
  • Gently release it.
  • After letting it go, REJOICE — remind yourself such thoughts are not truly part of you.
  • Begin mornings by acknowledging your place in this world, accepting that both good and bad exist.
  • Return to your breath. Your life becomes your practice.
"Hosh dar dam" — Breath Awareness. Make the breath your home base.
Go there whenever your thinking has no meaningful occupation.
• • •

Sky of Mind Practice

A core practice bridging Shamatha (calm abiding), Vipassana (insight), and Sufi contemplation.

Stage 1 — Witnessing

Identify with the Sky of Mind. Watch impressions inject, vibrate, fuse, shift, and scatter — just as clouds form and dissolve. Do not suppress. Do not fully identify. Watch passively.

Stage 2 — Dark Clouds

When clouds are so dense that no clarity remains — a thousand proliferating shapes, endlessly whirling. Recognize this state without judgment.

Stage 3 — Remembering the Sun

This is precisely the moment to remember the light. Besides the clouds, there is the sun. The soul is the sun of the sky of the mind. Visualize the inner sun dawning, scattering the clouds, bathing the whole sky in warm glow.

Stage 4 — Luminous Clouds

Clouds may remain — but now of a higher order. Wisps of ice crystal that reflect and refract the soul's light in rainbow hues. The finer dimension of thought is not a veil but an illuminated medium. Being becomes luminous thought.

Stage 5 — Radiation

Open your eyes. Allow the sun of the sky of your mind to shine out through your glance and radiate upon all you see. See in everyone around you the sun of a soul shining out.

Additional Teachings

Notice and observe the arising of thought — simple identification of what the mind is doing.

When thoughts carry you away — notice that, too. Notice and rejoice. That is a victory.

If self-criticism arises, notice it and return to the breath.

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

Kabir used this practice throughout a noisy Indian masala movie — protecting the heart, not getting distracted, treating himself while the family watched.

• • •

Fikr — Subuhun / Quddusun

Stage: Preparatory — precedes the current leading practice

Fikr: Inhale Subuhun → Exhale Quddusun. Begin with 33 repetitions.

The Names

  • Subuhun — God as the Glorified One, the One who is Praised.
  • Quddusun — God as the most Holy, Sacred One. God is infinite Sacredness; the sacredness that saturates the universe enfolds your being.

Pir Zia's Teaching

Return to one's angelic awareness, turned toward God. This is the purification of consciousness from the profanity of everyday life.

Use Mansur's audio recording Subuhun Quddusun.m4a to find the melody and tone.

• • •

Fikr — Ya Fazl / Ya Fattah

Stage: Current Leading Practice (as of February 2026)

Fikr: Inhale Fazl → Exhale Fattah

The Names

  • Fazl (Al-Fazl) — Blessing, illumination. Murshid called this "the word of blessing." The attunement you carry inward.
  • Fattah (Al-Fattah) — The Opener, the Revealer of paths. Carries cosmic attunement into action in the world.

This practice carries your developing attunement to Fazl out into active engagement with the world.

Built on the foundation of all preceding practices:

Sabur/Wahhab (patience/blessing) → Subuhun/Quddusun (purification) → Fazl Mullah (devotion) → Fazl/Fattah (action)

Received: February 11, 2026.

Fikr — Ya Sabur-o / Ya Wahhab-o

Stage: First / Foundation Practice

Vocal: Ya Sabur-o — Ya Wahhab-o (11 or 33× daily, or as needed)

Fikr: Inhale Sabur-o → Exhale Wahhab-o

The Names

  • Sabur (As-Sabur) — The Patient One. Meeting life in a friendly way each morning. Open to life as it is, at ease with what it brings.
  • Wahhab (Al-Wahhab) — The Supreme Bestower. The freely abundant flow of blessings, creativity, innovation, new inspiration, and resilience.
"Sabur is meeting life in a friendly way each morning. Wahhab is the freely abundant flow of blessings and life: full of creativity, innovation, new inspiration, and resilience." — Pir Zia Inayat Khan, 2021

Recite 11×, 33×, 99×, or 101×. What matters most is dwelling deeply in the meaning, allowing the Divine Names to permeate your being.

• • •

Subuhun Quddusun Rabbanu — New Evening Practice

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

Arabic

Subuhun — Quddusun — Rabbana (wa Rabbul Malaika war-Ruh)

Meaning

  • Subuhun = utterly pure, utterly transcendent
  • Quddusun = the Holy One
  • Rabbana = our Lord

Instructions

  • Recite before sleep — 33 repetitions (sweet spot; 11 also fine)
  • David Lyot: "Don't do too many — it becomes vain repetition"
  • Take the energy into sleep; let it work through the night (winding the clock)

Mansur's Description

"A really, deeply, powerfully, wonderfully intoxicating practice that infuses you with beautiful energy." — Mansur

Purpose

  • Evening anchor practice
  • Infuses beautiful energy
  • Works through the night
  • Preparation for sleep

Ya Fazl, Ya Fattah — Breath Fikr Practice

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

Practice Instructions

  • Inhale: Fazl (no "Ya" in the silent breath version)
  • Exhale: Fattah (no "Ya")
  • Use "Ya Fazl, Ya Fattah" only when reciting aloud

Kabir's Breakthrough Visualization

  • Fazl = knocking on the doors of the heart (inhale)
  • Fattah = the door opening (exhale)

Mansur's Teaching on Fikr Momentum

  • Fazl = blessing / "word of illumination" (Murshid's term — deeper than blessing)
  • Fattah = opener; releasing the blessing outward into the world

"When you pause — when all your thinking stops — the Fikr just starts up. Because you have planted it there." — Mansur

  • Wahabu = generosity / the outflowing of divine qualities — "slow motion Fattah"

"There's peace in that. A lot of peace." — Mansur

Effects

  • Eliminates internal arguments
  • Brings peace
  • Removes urgency and impatience
  • Stops internal struggles

Divine Presence and Primordial Longing

December 12, 2025 · Mansur

The Sacred Hadith

"I was a hidden treasure and I longed to be known. Therefore I created the world that I might be known." — Hadith Qudsi

The Teaching

The world was created from primeval longing released into manifestation. That longing IS the Divine Presence.

Buddhist Parallel

Mansur asked a Rinpoche (Dzogchen tradition) what corresponds to the Divine Presence in Buddhism. After reflection: "Emptiness."

Buddhist emptiness = Divine Presence = fundamental reality of the cosmos.

Understanding

This teaching reveals that the Divine Presence is not separate from creation but is the very longing that brought creation into being. The longing to be known, to be experienced, to manifest - this is the Divine Presence itself, the driving force of all existence.

The correspondence with Buddhist emptiness points to the universal recognition across traditions of this fundamental reality that underlies all phenomena.

• • •

Ego, Patience, and the Jihad with the False Self

March 1, 2026 · Mansur

The Real Jihad

"The real jihad is the war with the false self." — Mansur

How to Meet the Ego's Pushback

  1. Recognize it
  2. Accept it — don't surrender, but acknowledge it as normal
  3. Notice without reacting

"Rejoice when you notice — you are waking up." — Mansur

The Role of Patience

Patience allows you to notice the ego's locomotive and not carelessly get on board and go wherever it goes.

Building the Spiritual Locomotive

"You're not just fighting the ego locomotive — you're building a locomotive that will swallow that one up." — Mansur

Guide Session — December 12, 2025

Mansur & Kabir | Journey with the Guide

Summarized: 2026-03-11

Session Overview

First one-on-one Zoom session between Kabir (Ramesh) and his guide Mansur following Kabir's entry into the Suluk Global 2025–2027 program. This session covered the Five Elemental Purification Breaths in deep detail, explored Kabir's spontaneous insight about light as the substrate of the elements, touched on the nature of the Divine Presence, and provided personal spiritual guidance around patience, maintaining center in the world, and the daily practice rhythm.

1. Five Elemental Purification Breaths — Full Instructions

Mansur gave detailed guidance on each of the five elemental breaths, including the direction, visualization, and the underlying spiritual principle.

Earth Breath
  • Principle: "Stones are bones and flesh is clay." Principle of solidity.
  • Direction: Spreading — outward in all directions, toward you.
  • Visualization: Imagine yourself as a mountain buried from the waist down; earth spreading toward you from every direction on the inhale.
  • Exhale: Allow gravity (the earth's embrace of us) to purify the earth element.
Water Breath
  • Principle: Pir Vilayat: this element is very close to the Holy Spirit.
  • Direction: Down.
  • Visualization: A waterfall starting right above the head — high volume, slow-moving water pouring over and rinsing through you, like a smooth river.
  • Note: Mansur noted this visualization resonates especially because of Kabir's own affinity with water in meditation.
Fire Breath
  • Principle: Pir Vilayat: "You must burn very hot."
  • Direction: Upward.
  • Breathing: Inhale through the mouth, exhale through the nose.
  • Visualization: A bed of glowing coals in the solar plexus/belly. Inhale like bellows into the coals — making them bright. Exhale as exhaling light. Convection currents rising above a fire can be included.
  • Experience: Breathing in fire, exhaling light.
Air Breath
  • Principle: We are 99.999...% open space at the atomic level.
  • Direction: Every which way / zigzag.
  • Breathing: In through the mouth, out through the mouth.
  • Visualization: Swirling wind washing over and through you. Wind blowing through trees, cleansing all the dust — the body being cleansed by wind passing through it.
Ether Breath
  • Principle: The all-pervading life in space.
  • Direction: No fixed direction — omnidirectional, pervading presence.
  • Visualization: Subtle; feel the shimmering energy in the body. The line from the Prayer of Niyaz applies: "Through the all-pervading life in space."
  • Connection: Divine Presence / Buddhist "Emptiness" (see section 3).
Prayer of Niyaz (closing the Elemental Breaths)
"Beloved Lord, Almighty God, through the rays of the sun, through the waves of the air, through the all-pervading life in space, purify and revivify us and we pray. Heal our bodies, hearts and souls."

2. Purification Breath — Health Benefits

  • Pir Vilayat's teaching: "If you do the purification breaths every day, you'll never get seriously ill."
  • Mansur's personal testimony: 25 years of daily practice; sick only twice — once under extreme life stress (selling home, business, divorce) and once briefly with COVID in 2022.
  • Encouragement to Kabir: Take up this practice. It reveals itself over time.

3. Kabir's Insight — Light as the Building Block of the Elements

"Light is the building block of the five elements. The densest is earth. As it transitions — earth to water, water to fire, fire to air, air to ether — the building blocks that hold everything together is light (Nur). Creation is the transition from the most subtle (light) to the densest. Realization is the reverse — from the densest back to the light."

Mansur's response:

  • "That's a very interesting pairing — sound and light. They go together."
  • "You can contemplate radiance being resonant and sound being luminous."
  • At a certain level of depth, they are one thing.
  • Both Buddhism and Sufism affirm: "It's all light." It doesn't mean it's not all sound also.
  • "We discover these things by doing what you're doing — contemplating, looking within, releasing yourself into that subtle world."

4. The Divine Presence and the Longing

Hadith Qudsi (Sufi tradition):

"I was a hidden treasure and I longed to be known. Therefore I created the world that I might be known."

The world was created from primeval longing released into manifestation. That longing is the Divine Presence.

Buddhist parallel:

  • Mansur asked a Rinpoche (Dzogchen tradition): "What in Buddhism corresponds to the Divine Presence?"
  • Rinpoche's answer after reflection: Emptiness.
  • Buddhist emptiness = the fundamental reality of the cosmos = Divine Presence (Sufi understanding).
  • "It's that longing that became the world."

5. Ego as Constructed Patterns — The AI Bot Metaphor

Mansur offered a framing for the psychological self:

"We have fabricated a whole host of bots — emotional and mental creatures — each assigned the task of managing different situations and challenges in our life, based on our history, our trauma. Like AI bots."

Kabir acknowledged childhood trauma. Mansur offered gentleness: include the patience of an older brother — "It's okay. You got this."

6. Patience — Ya Saburu — First Practice Given

Kabir's challenge:

  • At 50 years old: "I was born in 1976. I don't have time to waste. I need to achieve, to get to my goal."
  • Internal urgency: not the right mindset.

Mansur's guidance:

  • The path is not like a worldly task where you "put your nose to the grindstone."
  • "You actuate and then you allow."
  • Like winding a clock (Murshid's metaphor): every day the practices, every day a mini-retreat; adjustments in perspective accumulate invisibly over time.
  • Ya Saburu = practice of patience — the very first practice given.

7. Maintaining Center in the World — Breath as Anchor

Kabir's challenge: When going out (visiting a temple, family time) — losing the inner shield, the centeredness.

Mansur's guidance:

  • The breath is the key. Even in conversation, keep breath awareness: inhaling, exhaling — the simplest thread back.
  • Teaching from Shahabuddin (30 years ago, walking in downtown San Francisco): "Your breath is your anchor."
  • Think of the breath as your big brother: always there, unfailingly supporting you.

Sky of Mind practice — application:

  • Trains the ability to notice distraction and return to breath.
  • In the world, when you feel your groundedness slipping: go to the breath. Pause. You don't have to keep up with fast talkers.

8. Daily Practice as Winding a Clock

Murshid's teaching on daily practice:

"Every day you do your practices. Every day the breathing exercises. It's like winding a clock — like a mini-retreat. There's some little shift happening each time, whether you recognize it or not. You're out in the world afterward, and everything's a little different."

Kabir's confirmation: "Each day is a new experience. New revelations every time I say the invocation — a different meaning each time."

Mansur: "That's what happens with a contemplative approach — a progressive unveiling of meanings and perceptions."

9. Kabir's Writings — Bowl of Saki Style

  • Kabir has been writing short pieces in the style of Bowl of Saki — approximately 70 writings.
  • Shared with Pir Zia.
  • Mansur expressed interest in receiving these writings.
  • Mansur mentioned writing haiku himself as a spiritual practice.

Practices Active at This Stage

Key Quotes

"Your breath is your anchor." — Shahabuddin (through Mansur)
"You actuate and then you allow." — Mansur
"I was a hidden treasure and I longed to be known. Therefore I created the world." — Hadith Qudsi
"You can contemplate radiance being resonant and sound being luminous." — Mansur
"Every day you do your practices. It's like winding a clock." — Murshid (through Mansur)
"It's okay. You got this. It's difficult, some of it's painful, but it's okay." — Mansur

Personal Guidance for Kabir

  • Include the patience of the great being from your dream walking through the desert.
  • Your determination and enthusiasm are a great help — add patience.
  • Feel free to email any experiences, progress, puzzlement.
  • Surgery note: Mansur had surgery that week — reconnect in January.

5. Elemental Breaths and Qasab — Observed Benefits

Kabir's testimony (7 rounds daily):

  • With practice: energy elevated, channels clear, clarity of thought restored
  • "It's like medicine. I can see the change."

Mansur's framework:

  • Elemental Breaths = purification of the body
  • Qasab = purification of the mind
  • Both clear the channels of breath (Nadis — 70,000 in yoga tradition)
  • Can do 1 round even in the car before a difficult meeting

6. Qasab — Breath Retention Version

Instructions
  1. Close one nostril. Inhale fully (e.g. 4 beats)
  2. Hold for 2× the inhale (e.g. 8 beats) — "Eight beats on either side. Think of it as a circle."
  3. Exhale for the inhale count (e.g. 4 beats)
  4. Repeat on other side

Kabir's full current sequence:

  1. Elemental breaths (7 rounds each)
  2. Simple alternating: in left/out right, in right/out left
  3. Inhale through mouth, exhale through nose
  4. Qasab with breath retention (4:8:4)

Caution: Do not force breath holding. Full breath, not excessive. Max 10 rounds (Murshid).

Teach us Thy loving forgiveness, raise us above the distinctions and differences which divide us. Send us the peace of Thy divine spirit, and unite us all in Thy perfect being. Om."

Practices & Teachings — Consolidated

Mansur (John Rahm) → Kabir (Ramesh)

Inayatiyya Order · December 2025 – March 2026

"It can't be taught, but it can be caught." — Mansur

Contents

#Section
IFoundational Orientation
IIBreathing Practices
IIIFikr — Divine Names on the Breath
IVSky of Mind
VDaily Practice Sequence
VISacred Relationship & Journey

I. Foundational Orientation

1.1 — Invocation: The Inner State

When reciting the invocation, bring forth the intention and feeling of longing — of union with the One. Without this inner state, the invocation becomes only a repetition of words.

1.2 — Watchfulness of Thought (Muraqabah)
  • Be watchful of thoughts. When a negative thought arises, pause — where is this coming from? Gently release it.
  • After letting it go, REJOICE — such thoughts are not truly part of you.
  • Begin mornings acknowledging your place in the world.
  • Return to the breath. Your life becomes your practice.
Hosh dar dam — Breath Awareness. Make the breath your home base.
1.3 — Ideal Guru Contemplation

Hold the archetype of the Ideal Guru — the being of Pir Zia, the yogi who blessed you. This beingness is contagious — it can be caught, not just taught.

II. Breathing Practices

2.1 — Five Elemental Purification Breaths

Daily, outside or open window. 5 breaths per element. Full, even, natural, unforced.

ElementPatternDirectionColorQualities
EarthIn nose · Out noseHorizontalGold wheatPatience, quiet strength
WaterIn nose · Out mouthDescendingSea greenGenerosity, creativity
FireIn mouth · Out noseRisingRedArdor, passion, truth
AirIn mouth · Out mouthZigzagSky bluePeace
EtherIn+out both simultaneouslyWithinUltra-fine grey mistLetting go
2.2 — Qasab: Cleansing the Chalice (Preliminary)

Hand position: Right middle finger on right nostril, right thumb under chin. Left hand covers right, left thumb closes left nostril. Light but deep breath, no strain.

  1. Close right → inhale/exhale left
  2. Close left → inhale/exhale right
  3. Close right; inhale left → close left; exhale right
  4. Close left; inhale right → close right; exhale left
  5. Close right; inhale left → exhale mouth
  6. Close left; inhale right → exhale mouth
  7. Close right; inhale mouth → exhale left
  8. Close left; inhale mouth → exhale right
  9. Lower hands

Repeat 1, 3, 5, or 7 rounds.

2.3 — Qasab: Standard Version

Deep inhale without strain. At top: close nostrils, chest expanded (no glottis closure), curl tongue on roof of mouth, eyeballs upward. Hold 2× inhale count thinking HU (or Allah inhale / Hu exhale).

Pattern: Inhale left × counts → hold 2× → exhale right (3×), then inhale right → exhale left (3×), then inhale both → exhale both (3×).

Begin with 3 of each. Increase gradually to 5, then 10. Never exceed 20. Take months. Never strain.

III. Fikr — Divine Names on the Breath

Recitation is internal, on the breath. Drop "Ya" prefix in breath-based fikr.

Fikr Progression
StagePracticeInhaleExhaleTheme
FirstYa Sabur-o / Ya Wahhab-oSabur-oWahhab-oFoundation
PreparatorySubuhun / QuddusunSubuhunQuddusunPurification
BlessingFazl Mullah Rahim Allah——Devotion
CurrentYa Fazl / Ya FattahFazlFattahAction
AdvancedAyat al-Qutb——Advanced
3.1 — Ya Sabur-o / Ya Wahhab-o (First Practice)
  • Sabur — The Patient One. Meeting life in a friendly way each morning.
  • Wahhab — The Supreme Bestower. Freely abundant blessings, creativity, innovation, resilience.
  • Vocal: 11 or 33×. Fikr: Inhale Sabur-o → Exhale Wahhab-o.
3.2 — Subuhun / Quddusun (Preparatory)
  • Subuhun — God as the Glorified One, the One who is Praised.
  • Quddusun — God as the most Holy, Sacred One. The sacredness that saturates the universe.
  • Fikr: Inhale Subuhun → Exhale Quddusun. Begin with 33×.
  • Use Mansur's audio recording to receive the melody.
3.3 — Fazl Mullah Rahim Allah (Blessing Invocation)
  • Fazl = Blessing · Mullah = Great · Rahim Allah = Allah's Compassion
  • Can be sung or recited. Use Mansur's audio recording.
3.4 — Ya Fazl / Ya Fattah (Current Leading Practice)
  • Fazl — Blessing, illumination. The word of blessing (Murshid).
  • Fattah — The Opener, the Revealer of paths. Attunement into action.
  • Fikr: Inhale Fazl → Exhale Fattah.
3.5 — Ayat al-Qutb (Advanced — Preliminary Version)

Preceded by Subuhun-Quddusun as preparatory practice. Shared by Mansur, March 6, 2026.

Reference: Gatha II, Pasi Anfas — wahiduddin.net/mv2/XIII/XIII_11.htm

IV. Sky of Mind

Core and foundational. Bridges Shamatha, Vipassana, and Sufi contemplation.

4.1 — Brief Form

Notice thought → Identify ("planning", "memory") → Release → REJOICE → Return to patient waiting.

4.2 — Full Five-Stage Practice (Pir Zia)
  1. Witnessing — Identify with the Sky of Mind. Watch impressions form and dissolve like clouds.
  2. Dark Clouds — When clarity is impossible. Clouds dense and chaotic.
  3. Remembering the Sun — The soul is the sun of the sky of the mind. Visualize it dawning.
  4. Luminous Clouds — Higher clouds of ice crystal wisps, refracting the soul's light in rainbow hues.
  5. Radiation — Open eyes. Let the soul's sun radiate through your glance upon all you see.

V. Daily Practice Sequence

TimePractice
MorningFive Elemental Purification Breaths
MorningInvocation (with longing)
Morning/DailyYa Sabur-o / Ya Wahhab-o (11 or 33×)
PreparatorySubuhun Quddusun Fikr (33×)
LeadingYa Fazl / Ya Fattah Fikr
ThroughoutWatchfulness of Thought · Hosh dar dam · REJOICE
As neededQasab (Cleansing the Chalice)
DedicatedSky of Mind Contemplation
"Not every practice we take up will land, and that's normal and ok." — Mansur

Light as Building Block of Five Elements

December 12, 2025 · Mansur

Kabir's Insight

"Light (Nur) is the building block of the five elements. Creation = subtle to dense. Realization = dense to subtle, back to the light." — Kabir

Mansur's Response

  • Sound and light go together — they are a natural pairing
  • "You can contemplate radiance being resonant and sound being luminous." — Mansur
  • At a certain level of depth, they are one thing

The Teaching

This insight reveals the fundamental substrate of creation - that light (Nur) is the primordial substance from which the five elements condense. The path of realization involves tracing this manifestation back from dense to subtle, ultimately returning to the original light.

The pairing of light and sound as complementary aspects of the same fundamental reality points to their unity at the deepest level of experience.

Place fingertips on device and recite blessing to sanctify phones/computers as spiritual artifacts.
